[09/02/24, 12:34:56 PM] Sachin Silas: Despite phenomenal advances in science, technology, medicine and agricultural production, the promise that ‘development’ would eradicate world poverty remains unfulfilled in several parts of the globe, especially in the third world. ‘Progress’ has come at a price: global warming, ozone depletion, loss of biodiversity, soil erosion, air and water pollution are all global problems with wide-ranging impacts on human populations, impacts that are significantly more harmful for the rural poor in third world countries, and for people who derive their sustenance from the land.

Experiencing the effects of climate change firsthand, such as the absence of snow in Gulmarg during mid-January, can indeed be a profound awakening to the urgency of sustainable development. The Himalayan region, renowned for its breathtaking landscapes and unique ecosystems, is particularly vulnerable to the impacts of climate change. The situation I witnessed, with uncharacteristically dry winters and reduced snowfall, underscores the importance of taking action to mitigate climate change and promote sustainable practices. From disrupted ecosystems to impacts on local livelihoods and economies dependent on tourism, the consequences of a changing climate are far-reaching and demand urgent attention. It's moments like these that often inspire individuals to become advocates for change, whether through personal choices, community initiatives, or broader advocacy efforts.

Over the span of two years, my voluntary work within diverse communities has significantly deepened my interest in the development sector. This burgeoning passion has solidified my resolve to pursue a career in sustainable development. Exposure to environmental issues during my academic journey, particularly discussions on climate change, spurred my awareness of global challenges. My research papers in Political Science and Geography delved into crucial topics such as the deforestation of the Amazon rainforest, actions by organizations like the World Meteorological Organization, major contributors to global greenhouse gas production, and solutions for reducing CO2 emissions through sustainable food management and smart city planning.  

Determined to learn more about the field, I managed to attend a board meeting, providing me with a first-hand experience of the necessity of merging economic profit with environmental sustainability, and adding to my thirst for further studies within the field. My vision is to contribute to the transformation of my society through sustainable interventions that facilitate positive change in the lives of vulnerable populations and achieve the Sustainable Development Goals (SDGs) set for 2030. In my community, numerous challenges persist, but there is a lack of individuals equipped with the necessary skill sets to critically analyze and devise sustainable solutions. Frequently, community interventions lack longevity, are misdirected, and eventually abandoned by beneficiaries due to their unsustainability.    

In the context of India, shaped by its status as a postcolonial nation-state, the trajectory of its planning and development processes has been heavily influenced by the ideals of modernity. However, this path has often led to unintended consequences, including development-induced violence and dispossession. Today, India faces a critical challenge: to glean valuable lessons from the missteps of more developed nations and craft policy frameworks that not only mitigate the negative impacts of development but also foster sustainable avenues for economic growth. By embracing a nuanced understanding of development—one that acknowledges the interconnectedness of social, economic, and environmental factors—India can chart a more equitable and sustainable path forward. This journey entails not only learning from past mistakes but also actively engaging with diverse stakeholders to co-create solutions that promote inclusive prosperity and safeguard the planet for future generations. 

I aim to reclaim the term "development" in practice, so as to it means what it was supposed to mean, which in the current scenario has been reduced to a mere buzzword in policy framework.
